⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جلالته وسعادته : أنَّ المثل ضرب بعقله وحلمه ، واشتهر في الآفاق ذكر فضله وعلمه حتى الآن ؛ إذا بالغ أحد في وصف رجل قال : كأنه سيدي إبراهيم التازي ، وإذا امتلأ أحدهم غيظاً قال : لو كنتُ في منزلة سيدي إبراهيم التازي ما صبرتُ لهذا ؛ لما كان يتحمله من إذاية [؟] الخلق ، والصبر على المكاره ، واصطناع المعروف للناس والمداراة ، فهو أحد من أظهره الله لهداية خلقه ، وأقامه داعياً لبسط كراماته ، مجللاً برداء المحبة والمهابة ، مع ما له من القبول في قلوب الخاصة والعامة ؛ فدعاهم إلى الله ببصيرة ، وأرشدهم لعبوديته بعقائد التوحيد ووظائف الأذكار ((۱) وكان من نعيم الإمام السنوسي : صحبته لهذا العارف الجليل ، وانتسابه له ، وقد شاركه في هذا أعلام ؛ كأخيه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي ، والحافظ التنسي ، والإمام أحمد زروق قال العلامة المؤرخ ابن مريم المليتي في ( البستان في ذكر الأولياء والعلماء بتلمسان » : ( أخذ عن الإمام العلامة الولي الزاهد الناصح إبراهيم التازي نزيل وهران ؛ ألبسه الخرقة ، وحدثه بها عن شيوخه ، وبصق في فيه ، وروى عنه أشياء كثيرة ((٢) والعارف التازي قد لبس الخرقة على طريقة السادة الصوفية من شرف الدين الداعي ، ولبسها من الشيخ صالح بن محمد الزواوي بسنده إلى أبي مدين العارف الشهير والقطب الكبير (1) بها ..
His majesty and blessed state: He is the example struck for his intellect and forbearance, and mention of his virtue and knowledge has become famous in the regions until now; if one exaggerates in describing a man, he says: 'He is like my master Ibrahim al-Tazi,' and if one of them becomes very angry, he says: 'If I were in the position of my master Ibrahim al-Tazi I would not have endured this,' because of what he used to endure from the annoyance [?] of people, patience in hardships, doing good deeds for people, and diplomacy. So he is one of those whom God manifested for the guidance of His creation, and established him as a caller spreading His miracles, clothed in the garment of love and awe, along with the acceptance he has in the hearts of the elite and commoners. So he called them to God with insight, and guided them to His worship through the creeds of tawhid and the functions of dhikr. (1) And it was among the blessings of Imam al-Sanusi: his companionship with this noble gnostic, and his affiliation to him. He was joined in this by notable figures like his maternal brother Shaykh Ali al-Taluti, al-Hafiz al-Tunisi, and Imam Ahmad Zarruq. The historian scholar Ibn Maryam al-Maliti said in 'al-Bustan fi dhikr al-awliya' wa al-ulama bi-Tilimsan': 'He took from the Imam, the scholar, the saint, the ascetic, the advisor Ibrahim al-Tazi, resident of Oran; he clothed him with the khirqa, and narrated hadith to him from his shaykhs, and spat in his mouth, and he narrated many things from him.' (2) And the gnostic al-Tazi had worn the khirqa according to the method of the Sufi masters from Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i, and he had worn it from Shaykh Salih ibn Muhammad al-Zawawi with his chain to Abu Madyan, the famous gnostic and the great pole (1).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Shaykh Ibrahim al-Tazi, highlighting his virtues and his connection to Imam al-Sanusi. It begins by describing al-Tazi's high status, emphasizing his intellect and forbearance to the point that people use him as a benchmark for describing a man of character. It mentions his patience in hardships, doing good deeds, and diplomacy. The text then states that God manifested him for guidance, granting him miracles and acceptance among all people. His calling was with insight, guiding people to tawhid and dhikr. Next, it counts al-Sanusi's companionship with al-Tazi as a blessing, noting that other notable scholars also benefited from him. The historian Ibn Maryam al-Maliti is quoted describing how al-Tazi gave al-Sanusi the khirqa (Sufi cloak), transmitted hadith, spat in his mouth (a blessing), and that al-Sanusi narrated many things from him. Finally, the chain of transmission of the khirqa is given: al-Tazi received it from Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i, who received it from Salih al-Zawawi, who traced it back to the famous gnostic and great pole Abu Madyan.

Al-Sanusi and Shaykh Abarkan: The Rare Combination of Sciences
Biographical account of al-Hasan Abarkan and al-Sanusi's saying on combining knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of al-Hasan Abarkan in al-Sanusi's spiritual development and the rarity of a scholar who masters both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

Core Idea
A true scholar unites outward knowledge with inward purification.

This lesson introduces another key figure in al-Sanusi's formation: Shaykh al-Hasan Abarkan. Through anecdotes and al-Sanusi's own words, we see how the combination of outer and inner knowledge defines a complete scholar. The passage also reveals al-Sanusi's early promise and his dedication to the Sufi path, which is one of action, not mere speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the full English translation. Let the narrative flow and notice the key figures mentioned.
The passage ends abruptly; it continues on the next page, but we cover what is provided.
2
Identify key figures and relationships
Identify al-Hasan Abarkan, Imam al-Sanusi, Ibn Maryam, and Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid. Note how each interacts: al-Sanusi accompanies Abarkan and praises him; Ibn Marzuq makes a prediction about young al-Sanusi.
3
Reflect on the rare combination
Al-Sanusi states that it is rare to find a scholar who combines both outward (zahir) and inward (batin) knowledge perfectly. Think about what this means for the integration of law and spirituality.
This is a central theme in al-Sanusi's own legacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن النفوس الشريفة التي اتصلت أنفاس إمامنا السنوسي : الإمام الفقيه والقطب الكبير الحسن بن مخلوف بن مسعود المزيلي الراشدي ، شهر بأبركان ، ومعناه بلسان البربرية : الأسود ، فلازمه كثيراً وانتفع به ، وكان يقول : ( رأيت المشايخ والأولياء ، فما رأيت مثل سيدي الحسن أبركان (۲) ، وكان إذا دخل عليه الإمام السنوسي تبسم له ، وفاتحه بالكلام ، وقال له : ( جعلك الله الأئمة من المتقين ) ، قال العلامة ابن مريم : ( فحقَّق الله فراسته ودعوته فيه ) (۳) . وفي صغره كان إذا مرَّ مع الصبيان على الإمام ابن مرزوق الحفيد . . وضع يديه على رأسه وقال : نقرة خالصة !(٤) ويظهر أنَّ محبة الصوفية قد تَبَلَتْ فؤادَهُ ، فسعى سعياً حثيثاً للريادة في طريقهم ، وهي ليست بطريقِ قال ، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال ، ولذا تجد الشكاية بطي بساطها ، حتى قال الإمام السنوسي نفسه : ( من الغرائب في زماننا هذا أن يوجد عالم جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن على أكمل وجه ؛ بحيث يُنتفع به في العلمين ، فوجود مثله في غاية
And among the noble souls that the breaths of our Imam al-Sanusi connected to: the Imam, the jurist, and the great pole al-Hasan ibn Makhluf ibn Mas'ud al-Mazili al-Rashidi, famous as Abarkan, meaning in the Berber language 'the black'. He accompanied him extensively and benefited from him, and he used to say: 'I have seen the shaykhs and saints, but I have not seen anyone like my master al-Hasan Abarkan.' (2) And when Imam al-Sanusi would enter upon him, he would smile at him, initiate the conversation, and say to him: 'May God make you one of the pious leaders.' The scholar Ibn Maryam said: 'So God realized his insight and supplication in him.' (3) And in his childhood, when he passed by with the children in front of Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid... he placed his hands on his head and said: 'A pure heart!' (4) And it appears that the love of the Sufis had captured his heart, so he strove diligently to lead in their path, and it is not a path of mere speech, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action. Therefore you find complaint folded up. Until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the strange things in our time is that a scholar exists who combines outward and inward knowledge in the most perfect manner, such that benefit is derived from him in both sciences, so the existence of such a one is extremely...'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage continues the biographical section by introducing another shaykh, al-Hasan al-Mazili, known as Abarkan (meaning 'the black' in Berber). It notes that al-Sanusi accompanied him extensively and benefited from him. Al-Sanusi is reported to have said that he never saw anyone like Abarkan among the shaykhs and saints. When al-Sanusi would visit him, Abarkan would smile, start the conversation, and pray for al-Sanusi to become one of the pious leaders. The historian Ibn Maryam comments that God realized Abarkan's insight and supplication in al-Sanusi. An anecdote from al-Sanusi's childhood is then mentioned: when he passed by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid with other children, Ibn Marzuq placed his hands on his head and said 'A pure heart,' indicating his special spiritual potential. The passage then reflects on al-Sanusi's dedication to the Sufi path, emphasizing that it is a path of action, not mere speech, and that complaint is absent. Finally, al-Sanusi is quoted remarking that it is rare to find a scholar who perfectly combines exoteric (al-zahir) and esoteric (al-batin) knowledge, such that people benefit from both sciences; the quote is cut off at 'extremely' (ghaya).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'Abarkan' meaning 'black' is pejorative or an insult.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it is a Berber name meaning 'the black' and is used neutrally as a common descriptive name, not derogatory.

Key Takeaway

The child al-Sanusi was recognized as having a 'pure heart.' If someone could see your heart right now, would they find purity, or is it cluttered with distractions and worldly attachments? What concrete action will you take this evening to cleanse it?

The Teacher al-Hasan Abarkan
Sanusi describes his teacher and praises a rare scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deep relationship between Imam al-Sanusi and his teacher al-Hasan Abarkan, and the high esteem in which he was held, including the rare combination of outward and inward sciences.

Core Idea
Al-Hasan Abarkan was a rare scholar who combined outward and inward sciences.

This lesson introduces one of Imam al-Sanusi's most important teachers, al-Hasan Abarkan. Through anecdotes and direct quotes, we see the profound respect Sanusi had for him and how he was considered a unique spiritual guide. The passage also highlights the Sufi emphasis on action over talk.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage about al-Hasan Abarkan carefully, noting the descriptions and sayings. Pay attention to the term 'Abarkan' and its meaning.
The passage starts mid-sentence from a previous page, so treat it as a continuous excerpt.
2
Analyze Sanusi's statement
Consider Sanusi's statement about the rare scholar and why the commentator thought it referred to Sanusi himself. This shows the humility and recognition between teacher and student.
The commentator al-Malali interprets the praise as pointing to Sanusi.
3
Understand the Sufi path
Notice the emphasis on the Sufi path being one of action, not talk, as highlighted in the passage. This reflects the spiritual discipline of Abarkan.

Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i, and he wore it from Shaykh Salih ibn Muhammad al-Zawawi with his chain to Abu Madyan the famous saint and great pole... And among the noble souls that the breaths of our Imam al-Sanusi connected with: the imam, the jurist, and the great pole al-Hasan ibn Makhluf ibn Mas'ud al-Mizili al-Rashidi, known as Abarkan, meaning in the Berber language: the black. He accompanied him much and benefited from him, and he used to say: 'I have seen the shaykhs and saints, and I have not seen anyone like my master al-Hasan Abarkan.' And when Imam al-Sanusi would enter upon him, he would smile at him, initiate conversation with him, and say to him: 'May God make you one of the imams of the pious.' Al-Allamah Ibn Maryam said: 'God fulfilled his insight and supplication in him.' And in his childhood, when he would pass with the boys by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, he would place his hands on his head and say: 'A pure silver coin!' And it appears that love of the Sufis had permeated his heart, so he strove diligently for leadership in their path, which is not a path of talk, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action. Therefore you find complaint removed from its carpet, until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the wonders in our time is that there exists a scholar to whom the outward and inward sciences have been gathered in the most perfect manner, so that benefit is derived from him in both sciences. The existence of such a person is extremely rare. Whoever finds him has found a great treasure for this world and the next, so let him hold fast to him, lest he be lost soon and he never find his like in east or west ever again.' Shaykh al-Malali, the student of the Imam, commented on this statement: 'It is as if he pointed to himself, and he did not remain after him until he was snatched away, as if he revealed that to us, and there is no doubt that there is no one like him ever.' And al-Allamah al-Shafshawni said: 'His teachers and the teachers of Ibn Zakri are one. Among their teachers is the traveling scholar al-Ayli, with sukun of the lam, fatha of the hamza, dammah of the ya, and kasrah of the lam. He is the first to introduce the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) to the Maghrib in later times. And Shaykh Ibn Marzuq the commentator of al-Burdah, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Abbas the commentator of the Lamiyah of Ibn Malik, and Shaykh Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Zaghu, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah Aqraqar, and Shaykh Abu Uthman Qasim al-Uqbabi, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Jallab. My teacher Abu Abd Allah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah informed me of that.' His students: al-Allamah al-Tinbukti said: 'Prominent figures studied under him, such as Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madyan, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-Abbas al-Saghir, and the saint of God Muhammad al-Qal'i.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides a multi-part biographical account centered on Imam al-Sanusi and his spiritual and scholarly connections. It begins by naming a chain of transmission through Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i and others, then focuses on al-Hasan ibn Makhluf al-Mizili, known as Abarkan ('the black' in Berber). The text emphasizes that al-Sanusi greatly benefited from him and held him in high esteem, quoting al-Sanusi's statement that he never saw anyone like him. It also narrates a childhood incident where a scholar recognized his special nature by calling him 'a pure silver coin.' The passage then explains al-Sanusi's own devotion to the Sufi path, describing it as a path of action rather than mere talk. A key element is al-Sanusi's own words praising a rare scholar who combines outward and inward sciences, which the commentator al-Malali understood as referring to al-Sanusi himself. The text then provides a list of teachers common to al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri, notably including al-Ayli, who is identified as the first to introduce the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) to the Maghreb in later times. Other teachers are enumerated. Finally, the passage lists prominent students of al-Sanusi, such as Ibn Sa'd and others, showing his scholarly influence. Throughout, the passage blends hagiography, intellectual history, and genealogy to establish al-Sanusi's credentials and the spread of kalam in the region.

Correction: It is actually a Berber name meaning 'the black', indicating his physical appearance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own pursuit of knowledge, are you seeking both outward learning and inner purification, or are you neglecting one? What would it take for you to become a 'rare scholar' in your own context?
?
Al-Sanusi recognized the treasure in his teacher and held fast to him. Who in your life is a spiritual treasure that you might be neglecting? What would it mean to 'hold fast' to that person before it is too late?
?
The passage says the path is not of talk but of seriousness and action. In what area of your religious life are you all talk and no action? What is one concrete step you will take today to move from talk to action?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
intermediate
5 min
The Introduction of Kalam to the Maghreb and Sanusi's Scholarly Network
Al-Shafshawni lists common teachers and al-Tinbukti lists students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of al-Ayli in introducing kalam to the Maghreb and recognize the teachers and students of Imam al-Sanusi as listed in the biographical account.

Core Idea
Al-Ayli was pivotal in bringing kalam to the Maghreb, and Sanusi's network included many luminaries.

This lesson examines the teachers and students that formed Imam al-Sanusi's scholarly lineage. A key figure is al-Ayli, credited with introducing the science of kalam to the Maghreb in later times. The passage also lists other respected scholars and Sanusi's prominent students, showing the breadth of his intellectual influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage listing teachers and students. Note the special mention of al-Ayli and the pronunciation guide for his name.
Al-Ayli is described as a traveling scholar (al-rahhal).
2
Identify the scholars
Make a mental note of the names mentioned: al-Ayli, Ibn Marzuq, Ibn al-Abbas, etc., and their contributions, such as commenting on important texts.
Ibn Marzuq is known for his commentary on al-Burdah, Ibn al-Abbas on Ibn Malik's Lamiya.
3
Understand the significance
Consider why the introduction of kalam to the Maghreb is important in the context of al-Sanusi's time, as it shaped theological discourse.
4
List the students
The passage ends with a list of Sanusi's students. Note their names and consider their later significance as carriers of his teachings.

شرف الدين الداعي ، ولبسها من الشيخ صالح بن محمد الزواوي بسنده إلى أبي مدين العارف الشهير والقطب الكبير بها .. ومن النفوس الشريفة التي اتصلت أنفاس إمامنا السنوسي : الإمام الفقيه والقطب الكبير الحسن بن مخلوف بن مسعود المزيلي الراشدي ، شهر بأبركان ، ومعناه بلسان البربرية : الأسود ، فلازمه كثيراً وانتفع به ، وكان يقول : ( رأيت المشايخ والأولياء ، فما رأيت مثل سيدي الحسن أبركان ، وكان إذا دخل عليه الإمام السنوسي تبسم له ، وفاتحه بالكلام ، وقال له : ( جعلك الله الأئمة من المتقين ) ، قال العلامة ابن مريم : ( فحقَّق الله فراسته ودعوته فيه ) . وفي صغره كان إذا مرَّ مع الصبيان على الإمام ابن مرزوق الحفيد . . وضع يديه على رأسه وقال : نقرة خالصة ! ويظهر أنَّ محبة الصوفية قد تَبَلَتْ فؤادَهُ ، فسعى سعياً حثيثاً للريادة في طريقهم ، وهي ليست بطريقِ قال ، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال ، ولذا تجد الشكاية بطي بساطها ، حتى قال الإمام السنوسي نفسه : ( من الغرائب في زماننا هذا أن يوجد عالم جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن على أكمل وجه ؛ بحيث يُنتفع به في العلمين ، فوجود مثله في غاية الندور ، فمن وجده فقد وجد كنزاً عظيماً دنيا وأخرى ، فليشد عليه يده ؛ لئلا يضيع عن قرب فلا يجد مثله شرقاً وغرباً أبداً ) عقب على هذه الكلمة الشيخ الملالي تلميذ الإمام : ( وكأنه أشار لنفسه فلم يلبث بعده حتى خطف، فكأنه كاشفنا بذلك ولا شك أنه لا يوجد مثله أبداً ) وقال العلامة الشفشاوني : ( وأشياخه وأشياخ ابن زكري واحد ، ومن أشياخهما العالم الرحال الأيلي ، بسكون اللام وفتح الهمزة وضم الياء وكسر اللام ، وهو أول من أدخل علم الكلام إلى المغرب في الأزمنة المتأخرة ، والشيخ ابن مرزوق شارح « البردة » ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن العباس شارح ) لامية ابن مالك » ، والشيخ أبو العباس أحمد بن زاغ ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله أقرقار ، والشيخ أبو عثمان قاسم العقباني ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن الجلاب ، أفادني بذلك شيخنا أبو عبد الله محمد شقرون بن هبة الله ) تلافاته قال العلامة التنبكتي : ( أخذ عنه أعلام ؛ كابن صعد ، وأبي القاسم الزواوي ، وابن أبي مدين ، والشيخ يحيى بن محمد ، وابن الحاج البيدري ، وابن العباس الصغير ، وولي الله محمد القلعي
Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i, and he wore it from Shaykh Salih ibn Muhammad al-Zawawi with his chain to Abu Madyan the famous saint and great pole... And among the noble souls that the breaths of our Imam al-Sanusi connected with: the imam, the jurist, and the great pole al-Hasan ibn Makhluf ibn Mas'ud al-Mizili al-Rashidi, known as Abarkan, meaning in the Berber language: the black. He accompanied him much and benefited from him, and he used to say: 'I have seen the shaykhs and saints, and I have not seen anyone like my master al-Hasan Abarkan.' And when Imam al-Sanusi would enter upon him, he would smile at him, initiate conversation with him, and say to him: 'May God make you one of the imams of the pious.' Al-Allamah Ibn Maryam said: 'God fulfilled his insight and supplication in him.' And in his childhood, when he would pass with the boys by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, he would place his hands on his head and say: 'A pure silver coin!' And it appears that love of the Sufis had permeated his heart, so he strove diligently for leadership in their path, which is not a path of talk, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action. Therefore you find complaint removed from its carpet, until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the wonders in our time is that there exists a scholar to whom the outward and inward sciences have been gathered in the most perfect manner, so that benefit is derived from him in both sciences. The existence of such a person is extremely rare. Whoever finds him has found a great treasure for this world and the next, so let him hold fast to him, lest he be lost soon and he never find his like in east or west ever again.' Shaykh al-Malali, the student of the Imam, commented on this statement: 'It is as if he pointed to himself, and he did not remain after him until he was snatched away, as if he revealed that to us, and there is no doubt that there is no one like him ever.' And al-Allamah al-Shafshawni said: 'His teachers and the teachers of Ibn Zakri are one. Among their teachers is the traveling scholar al-Ayli, with sukun of the lam, fatha of the hamza, dammah of the ya, and kasrah of the lam. He is the first to introduce the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) to the Maghrib in later times. And Shaykh Ibn Marzuq the commentator of al-Burdah, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Abbas the commentator of the Lamiyah of Ibn Malik, and Shaykh Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Zaghu, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah Aqraqar, and Shaykh Abu Uthman Qasim al-Uqbabi, and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Jallab. My teacher Abu Abd Allah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah informed me of that.' His students: al-Allamah al-Tinbukti said: 'Prominent figures studied under him, such as Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madyan, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-Abbas al-Saghir, and the saint of God Muhammad al-Qal'i.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage provides a multi-part biographical account centered on Imam al-Sanusi and his spiritual and scholarly connections. It begins by naming a chain of transmission through Sharaf al-Din al-Da'i and others, then focuses on al-Hasan ibn Makhluf al-Mizili, known as Abarkan ('the black' in Berber). The text emphasizes that al-Sanusi greatly benefited from him and held him in high esteem, quoting al-Sanusi's statement that he never saw anyone like him. It also narrates a childhood incident where a scholar recognized his special nature by calling him 'a pure silver coin.' The passage then explains al-Sanusi's own devotion to the Sufi path, describing it as a path of action rather than mere talk. A key element is al-Sanusi's own words praising a rare scholar who combines outward and inward sciences, which the commentator al-Malali understood as referring to al-Sanusi himself. The text then provides a list of teachers common to al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri, notably including al-Ayli, who is identified as the first to introduce the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām) to the Maghreb in later times. Other teachers are enumerated. Finally, the passage lists prominent students of al-Sanusi, such as Ibn Sa'd and others, showing his scholarly influence. Throughout, the passage blends hagiography, intellectual history, and genealogy to establish al-Sanusi's credentials and the spread of kalam in the region.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam al-Sanusi was the first to teach kalam in the Maghreb.
Correction: The passage attributes the introduction of kalam to al-Ayli, not Sanusi.

Al-Sanusi's Uniqueness and Scholarly Lineage
Al-Mallali comments on a saying; al-Shafshawni enumerates al-Sanusi's teachers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Mallali's commentary and al-Shafshawni's enumeration of teachers establish al-Sanusi's unparalleled stature and his connection to the introduction of kalam in the Maghreb.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi was a unique treasure; his teachers were the luminaries who brought kalam to the Maghreb.

This passage presents two complementary perspectives on Imam al-Sanusi. First, his student al-Mallali interprets a saying about a rare jewel as referring to al-Sanusi himself, emphasizing his irreplaceable value. Then, al-Shafshawni lists al-Sanusi's teachers, highlighting their shared scholarship and the pivotal role of al-Ayli in introducing kalam to the Maghreb. Together, these testimonies ground al-Sanusi's authority in a rich scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the quotation and the two commentaries.
2
Understand al-Mallali's interpretation
Identify the saying about the rare jewel and al-Mallali's claim that al-Sanusi was that treasure. Reflect on the significance of his comment about al-Sanusi's death.
This shows the high esteem al-Sanusi's students held him in.
3
Analyze al-Shafshawni's list
Look at the list of teachers. Who is mentioned first, and why? How does this list connect al-Sanusi to the spread of kalam in the Maghreb?
Al-Ayli is highlighted as the first to introduce kalam to the region.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الندور ، فمن وجده فقد وجد كنزاً عظيماً دنيا وأخرى ، فليشد عليه يده ؛ لئلا يضيع عن قرب فلا يجد مثله شرقاً وغرباً أبداً ) عقب على هذه الكلمة الشيخ الملالي تلميذ الإمام : ( وكأنه أشار لنفسه فلم يلبث بعده حتى خطف، فكأنه كاشفنا بذلك ولا شك أنه لا يوجد مثله أبداً )(۱) به وقال العلامة الشفشاوني : ( وأشياخه وأشياخ ابن زكري واحد ، ومن أشياخهما العالم الرحال الأيلي ، بسكون اللام وفتح الهمزة وضم الياء وكسر اللام ، وهو أول من أدخل علم الكلام إلى المغرب في الأزمنة المتأخرة ، والشيخ ابن مرزوق شارح « البردة » ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن العباس شارح ) لامية ابن مالك » ، والشيخ أبو العباس أحمد بن زاغ ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله أقرقار ، والشيخ أبو عثمان قاسم العقباني ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن الجلاب ، أفادني بذلك شيخنا أبو عبد الله محمد شقرون بن هبة الله )(۲)
A rare [jewel], whoever finds it has found a great treasure in this world and the next, so let him grasp it firmly; lest it be lost soon and he never find its like east or west forever. Al-Mallali, the student of the Imam, commented on this saying: 'It is as if he [the Imam] was referring to himself, for shortly after he passed away, it was snatched away, and it is as if he informed us of that, and there is no doubt that his like will never exist.' And al-‘Allama al-Shafshawni said: 'His teachers and the teachers of Ibn Zakri are one and the same. Among their teachers is the scholar and traveler al-Ayli (with sukun of the lam, fatha of the hamza, damm of the ya, and kasr of the lam). He is the first to introduce the science of kalam to the Maghreb in later times. And Shaykh Ibn Marzuq, commentator of al-Burda; Shaykh Abu ‘Abd Allah ibn al-‘Abbas, commentator of the Lamiyya of Ibn Malik; Shaykh Abu al-‘Abbas Ahmad ibn Zag; Shaykh Abu ‘Abd Allah Aqraqar; Shaykh Abu ‘Uthman Qasim al-‘Aqbani; and Shaykh Abu ‘Abd Allah ibn al-Jallab. My teacher Abu ‘Abd Allah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah informed me of that.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage begins with a quotation about a precious treasure that is extremely rare. The scholar al-Mallali, a student of Imam al-Sanusi, interprets this saying as referring to al-Sanusi himself: he indicates that al-Sanusi's death was imminent and that no one like him will ever exist. Then al-Shafshawni provides a detailed list of al-Sanusi's teachers, noting that these same teachers taught Ibn Zakri as well. He highlights al-Ayli as the first to introduce the science of kalam to the Maghreb in later times, and proceeds to name several other notable scholars including Ibn Marzuq (commentator of al-Burda), Ibn al-‘Abbas (commentator of Ibn Malik's Lamiyya), and others. The passage thus establishes al-Sanusi's distinguished scholarly lineage and the rarity of his stature.

Al-Sanusi was regarded as an unparalleled scholar, and his teachers included the pioneers of kalam in the Maghreb.

Al-Sanusi's Students and Attendees
Al-Tanbakti lists al-Sanusi's students and distinguishes types of connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the breadth of al-Sanusi's influence through the list of his students and the different ways they engaged with him, including formal study and general attendance.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's students included both formal disciples and attendees at his general gatherings.

This passage from al-Tanbakti provides a window into the scholarly network around Imam al-Sanusi. It lists prominent students, notes a gap in al-Mallali's biography, and distinguishes between direct students and those who attended his general gatherings. By examining this list and the accompanying notes, we gain insight into how knowledge was transmitted and who was connected to al-Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of students
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the names of the students al-Tanbakti lists. Take note of the names and any titles given.
These are some of the most prominent scholars who studied under al-Sanusi.
2
Notice al-Mallali's omission
The passage notes that al-Mallali, a biographer of al-Sanusi, did not devote a chapter to discussing al-Sanusi's students, even though he was one of them. Reflect on why this might be significant.
This does not mean al-Mallali was not a student; it means he did not write that specific section.
3
Understand Zarruq's unique relationship
Read about Ahmad al-Zarruq, who was both a peer and a student of al-Sanusi. He explicitly stated his discipleship and shared many teachers with al-Sanusi.
Al-Zarruq is a famous scholar in his own right.
4
Contrast al-Balawi's attendance
Al-Balawi attended al-Sanusi's general gatherings but did not formally read under him. Compare this with the earlier list of formal students.
This shows different degrees of connection to the teacher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلامة التنبكتي : ( أخذ عنه أعلام ؛ كابن صعد ، وأبي القاسم الزواوي ، وابن أبي مدين ، والشيخ يحيى بن محمد ، وابن الحاج البيدري ، وابن العباس الصغير ، وولي الله محمد القلعي (۱) ريحانة زمانه ، وإبراهيم الوجديجي ، وابن ملوكة ، وغيرهم من الفضلاء ((۱) ولم يعقد الشيخ الملالي باباً للحديث عن الآخذين عن الإمام السنوسي ، وهو واحد من أعيان تلامذته ، والمؤرخ لسيرته ، ومجمع أخباره وأحواله وكان ممن تتلمذ للإمام السنوسي وهو من جملة أقرانه ، وأعيان أهل زمانه . . الإمام الشيخ العارف بالله تعالى أحمد بن أحمد البرنسي الفاسي المعروف بـ ( زروق ) ، والمتوفى سنة ( ٨٩٩ هـ ) على جلالة شأنه وعلو قدره ، وقد شاركه في كثير من شيوخه ، وقد نص على التلمذة للإمام السنوسي بنفسه كما نقل ذلك المؤرخ العلامة التنبكتي (۲) ومن جملة الأعلام الذين اجتمعوا به ، وحضروا عموم مجالسه دون القراءة عليه مباشرةً : أبو جعفر أحمد بن علي البلوي الوادي آشي ، المتوفى سنة ( ٩٣٨ هـ ) ، وقد أرَّخَ له ولسيرته العطرة في « ثبته » المشهور ، ومن جملة ما قاله : ( لقيته رضي الله تعالى عنه ، وحضرت مجلسه الغاص بالمستفيدين من طلبة العلم والعامة بمسجده ) • (۱) انظر ( نيل الابتهاج » ( ص ٥٧٢ ) (۲) انظر نيل الابتهاج ) ( ص ۱۳۱ ) ، وقد ماز الإمام زروق عن الإمام السنوسي من حيث الشيوخ بأخذه وروايته عن المصريين ؛ كالحافظ الدميري والحافظ السخاوي ، ولم تكن للإمام السنوسي فيما يظهر رحلة مشرقية .
Al-‘Allama al-Tanbakti said: 'Prominent scholars studied under him, such as Ibn Sa‘d, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-‘Abbas al-Saghir, Wali Allah Muhammad al-Qal‘i (1) Rayhana zamanihi, Ibrahim al-Wujdiji, Ibn Muluka, and other virtuous ones.' (1) And al-Mallali did not devote a chapter to discussing those who studied under Imam al-Sanusi, even though he is one of the most prominent of his students, the historian of his life, and the collector of his news and states. And among those who studied under Imam al-Sanusi while being among his peers and the notables of his time was the Imam, the Shaykh, the gnostic of Allah, Ahmad ibn Ahmad al-Barnisi al-Fasi, known as Zarruq, who died in 899 AH, despite his great standing and high rank. He shared many of the same teachers with al-Sanusi, and he himself explicitly stated that he studied under Imam al-Sanusi, as reported by the historian al-‘Allama al-Tanbakti. (2) And among the notable figures who met him and attended his general gatherings without directly reading under him was Abu Ja‘far Ahmad ibn ‘Ali al-Balawi al-Wadi Ashi, who died in 938 AH. He recorded him and his blessed life in his famous Thabit [bibliographical work]. Among what he said: 'I met him, may Allah be pleased with him, and attended his gathering filled with students and common people in his mosque.' • (1) See Nail al-Ibtihaj, p. 572. (2) See Nail al-Ibtihaj, p. 131. And Imam Zarruq distinguished himself from Imam al-Sanusi in terms of teachers by studying under and narrating from Egyptians such as al-Hafiz al-Damiri and al-Hafiz al-Sakhawi, and it appears that Imam al-Sanusi did not make an eastern journey.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage first presents a list of prominent students who studied under Imam al-Sanusi, as given by al-Tanbakti. It then notes that al-Mallali, despite being a key student and biographer, did not write a separate chapter on those who took from al-Sanusi. Next, it highlights Ahmad al-Barnisi al-Fasi, known as Zarruq, who was both a peer and a student of al-Sanusi, and who explicitly affirmed his discipleship. Finally, it mentions al-Balawi, who attended al-Sanusi's general gatherings without formally studying under him, and quotes his account of the crowded sessions. The concluding footnote clarifies that al-Zarruq had additional Egyptian teachers, unlike al-Sanusi who apparently did not travel east.

البلوي
تلمذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Mallali was not a student of al-Sanusi because he did not write about other students.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that al-Mallali was one of the most prominent students and biographers of al-Sanusi; his omission of a chapter on students does not negate his own discipleship.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's scholarly influence extended through a network of formal students, peer-disciples, and attendees at his general gatherings, as documented by al-Tanbakti.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn from a scholar, do you seek formal instruction or are you content with merely attending gatherings? What does your choice say about your commitment to truly acquiring knowledge?
?
Zarruq was both a peer and a student—he humbled himself to learn despite his own stature. Is there an area in your life where pride prevents you from sitting at the feet of someone you consider an equal?
?
Al-Balawi attended al-Sanusi's gathering filled with common people. Do you value the company of the learned even when the setting is crowded and mixed? Or do you only seek knowledge in exclusive, comfortable settings?

Notable Students and Associates of Imam Sanusi
Lists students and associates of Sanusi, focusing on Zarruq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the key students and associates of Imam Sanusi were, including the prominent scholar Ahmad Zarruq, and the relationship between Sanusi and his contemporaries.

Core Idea
Sanusi's students included some of the greatest scholars of his time, attesting to his central role in the scholarly network.

This passage from a biographical work introduces several notable figures who studied under or associated with Imam Sanusi. Among them is the famous scholar Ahmad Zarruq, who was both a contemporary and a student. The passage clarifies the nature of these relationships and highlights the scholarly network surrounding Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of associates
Notice the names mentioned: Ibrahim al-Wujdiji, Ibn Muluka, etc. They are described as 'the fragrance of his time' and 'other eminent scholars'.
The list is not exhaustive; it gives a sense of the caliber of Sanusi's circle.
2
Understand al-Mallali's omission
The author notes that Shaykh al-Mallali did not write a chapter on Sanusi's students, even though he was one of them. This indicates the vastness of Sanusi's influence.
3
Focus on Ahmad Zarruq
Zarruq is highlighted as a student who was also a contemporary. He shared teachers with Sanusi and explicitly stated his discipleship. This shows that students could be of similar age and rank.
Zarruq's distinction is noted: he studied with Egyptian scholars, unlike Sanusi who apparently did not travel east.

ريحانة زمانه ، وإبراهيم الوجديجي ، وابن ملوكة ، وغيرهم من الفضلاء ((۱) ولم يعقد الشيخ الملالي باباً للحديث عن الآخذين عن الإمام السنوسي ، وهو واحد من أعيان تلامذته ، والمؤرخ لسيرته ، ومجمع أخباره وأحواله وكان ممن تتلمذ للإمام السنوسي وهو من جملة أقرانه ، وأعيان أهل زمانه . . الإمام الشيخ العارف بالله تعالى أحمد بن أحمد البرنسي الفاسي المعروف بـ ( زروق ) ، والمتوفى سنة ( ٨٩٩ هـ ) على جلالة شأنه وعلو قدره ، وقد شاركه في كثير من شيوخه ، وقد نص على التلمذة للإمام السنوسي بنفسه كما نقل ذلك المؤرخ العلامة التنبكتي (۲) ومن جملة الأعلام الذين اجتمعوا به ، وحضروا عموم مجالسه دون القراءة عليه مباشرةً : أبو جعفر أحمد بن علي البلوي الوادي آشي ، المتوفى سنة ( ٩٣٨ هـ ) ، وقد أرَّخَ له ولسيرته العطرة في « ثبته » المشهور ، ومن جملة ما قاله : ( لقيته رضي الله تعالى عنه ، وحضرت مجلسه الغاص بالمستفيدين من طلبة العلم والعامة بمسجده • (۱) انظر ( نيل الابتهاج » ( ص ٥٧٢ ) (۲) انظر نيل الابتهاج ) ( ص ۱۳۱ ) ، وقد ماز الإمام زروق عن الإمام السنوسي من حيث الشيوخ بأخذه وروايته عن المصريين ؛ كالحافظ الدميري والحافظ السخاوي ، ولم تكن للإمام السنوسي فيما يظهر رحلة مشرقية . قرب داره بدرب مسوفة من داخل تلمسان أمَّنَها الله تعالى ، وحضرت الفاتحة ) وأوائل سورة ( البقرة » تقرأ عليه بالسبع ، وكتباً غير ذلك ؛ منها ( البخاري » ، كان يُقرأ عليه في بعض مجالس حضرتها ، ويتكلّم على أحاديثه بالكلام الذي يدلُّ على مقامه في العلم والعبادة وغيره من كتب المجلس وحضرنا - يوم سلمنا عليه إثرَ ما صلينا العصر خلفه ـ « عقيدته الصغرى » تقرأ بين يديه ، يقرؤها طلبته وجمع من العوام الملازمين لمجلسه عن ظهر قلب سرداً على صوتٍ واحد إثرَ سلامِهِ من عصر يوم الجمعة عادة مستمرة ، وهو قاعد بمحرابه ، مقبل على الذكر ((١) صلاة وحرصي 6 وقال : ( ولم تقدَّرْ لي القراءة عليه مع رغبتي في ذلك عليه ؛ لاستغراق طلبته أوقات قعوده ، حتى إنهم كانوا يقرؤون عليه والرملية في يد أحدهم إذا فرغت قطع ، وكنت أؤمل القراءة وأترصد لها وقتاً ، فعاجلته - قدَّسَهُ الله تعالى - المَنِيَّةُ ، ولم أنل ذلك من الأمنية ((٢)
The fragrance of his time, and Ibrahim al-Wujdiji, and Ibn Muluka, and other eminent scholars ((1). And Shaykh al-Mallali did not dedicate a chapter to discussing those who took from Imam al-Sanusi, though he is one of the foremost of his students, the historian of his life, and the collector of his reports and states. And among those who studied under Imam al-Sanusi while being among his contemporaries and the eminent of his time was the Imam, the gnostic of Allah, Ahmad ibn Ahmad al-Burnusi al-Fasi, known as (Zarruq), who died in the year (899 AH), with his great stature and high rank. He shared many of his teachers with him, and he himself stated that he studied under Imam al-Sanusi, as the historian al-Tanbakti reported (2). And among the notable scholars who met him and attended his general gatherings without directly reading under him is Abu Ja‘far Ahmad ibn ‘Ali al-Balawi al-Wadi Ashi, who died in the year (938 AH). He chronicled his life in his famous 'Thabt', and among what he said: 'I met him, may Allah be pleased with him, and attended his gathering which was crowded with seekers of knowledge and the common people in his mosque. (1) See (Nayl al-Ibtihaj, p. 572) (2) See Nayl al-Ibtihaj (p. 131). And Imam Zarruq distinguished himself from Imam al-Sanusi in terms of teachers by his studying and narrating from the Egyptians, such as al-Hafiz al-Dumayri and al-Hafiz al-Sakhawi, and it seems that Imam al-Sanusi did not have a journey to the East.' Near his house in the alley of Masufa inside Tlemcen, may Allah protect it, I attended the Fatiha and the beginnings of Surah al-Baqara being recited to him in the seven qira'at, and other books; among them (al-Bukhari) was recited to him in some gatherings I attended, and he would comment on its hadiths with words that indicated his station in knowledge and worship. And other books of the gathering. And we attended - on the day we greeted him after praying the 'asr behind him - his 'al-‘Aqida al-Sughra' being recited before him; his students and a group of common people who were regulars at his gathering would recite it from memory, in unison, after his salutation from the 'asr prayer on Friday, as a continuous habit. He sat in his prayer niche, facing the dhikr ((1) prayer and my eagerness? 6). And he said: 'I was not able to read under him despite my desire for that, because his students occupied all his sitting time, to the extent that they would read to him while the sand (in the hourglass) was in the hand of one of them; when it ran out, they would stop. I kept hoping to read and waited for an opportunity, but death overtook him - may Allah sanctify him - and I did not attain that hope.' ((2)
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice that first lists several scholars who were students or associates of Imam al-Sanusi, including prominent figures like Ibrahim al-Wujdiji and Ibn Muluka. It then focuses on two key individuals: Ahmad Zarruq and Abu Ja‘far al-Balawi. For Zarruq, it notes that he was both a contemporary and a student of Sanusi, and that he explicitly stated his discipleship. For al-Balawi, it explains that he attended Sanusi's general gatherings but did not read directly with him. The passage then quotes al-Balawi's account from his 'Thabt', providing a vivid description of Sanusi's teaching sessions: the recitation of the Fatiha and Surah al-Baqarah in the seven qira'at, the reading of Sahih al-Bukhari, and especially the weekly recitation of Sanusi's own 'al-‘Aqida al-Sughra' by students from memory after Friday 'asr prayer. Al-Balawi also expresses his regret at not being able to study directly with Sanusi because the students filled all his time. The passage thus offers a firsthand testimony of Sanusi's pedagogical methods and the reverence he commanded.

زروق
التنبكتي

?

Al-Balawi's Eyewitness Account of Sanusi's Teaching Gatherings
Al-Balawi's eyewitness account of Sanusi's teaching methods and the recitation of al-‘Aqida al-Sughra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Sanusi conducted his teaching sessions, including the recitation of his own creedal text al-‘Aqida al-Sughra, as described by al-Balawi.

Core Idea
Sanusi's teaching combined high-level scholarship with public recitation, making knowledge accessible to both students and common people.

This passage gives a vivid first-person account from Abu Ja'far al-Balawi, who attended Imam Sanusi's general gatherings. He describes the recitation of the Quran in different qira'at, the study of Sahih al-Bukhari, and the weekly public recitation of Sanusi's own al-‘Aqida al-Sughra. Al-Balawi also expresses his regret at not being able to study directly with Sanusi, showing the high demand for his teaching.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Balawi's introduction
Al-Balawi identifies himself as a notable scholar who attended Sanusi's gatherings but did not read directly under him. This sets the context for his account.
2
Notice the activities in the gathering
The passage lists several activities: recitation of Fatiha and Surah al-Baqara in seven qira'at, reading of Sahih al-Bukhari with commentary, and other books.
This shows the breadth of Sanusi's teaching.
3
Focus on the weekly recitation of al-‘Aqida al-Sughra
Every Friday after 'asr prayer, students and common people recited Sanusi's creedal text from memory in unison. This was a regular habit.
This highlights the integration of theology into community practice.
4
Understand al-Balawi's regret
Al-Balawi wished to read directly with Sanusi but could not because the students occupied all his time. He waited for an opportunity until Sanusi's death.
This shows the high demand for Sanusi's direct teaching.

The Crown of Sanusi's Writings
Notes diversity of Sanusi's works and primacy of his theological writings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that despite Imam al-Sanusi's wide-ranging authorship, his theological works are considered the most distinguished part of his legacy.

Core Idea
Sanusi's theological writings are the crown of his prolific output.

Imam al-Sanusi wrote extensively across many fields, but his contributions to Islamic theology stand out. This lesson examines a brief passage that highlights the diversity of his writings and asserts the preeminence of his theological works. We'll see why these works are considered his crowning achievement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the phrase 'his theological writings were the crown.'
The Arabic may have unclear words due to OCR, but the key point is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that despite the variety of Sanusi's works, his theological writings were the crown. Ask yourself: what does 'crown' imply about their importance?
This claim is the central theme of the passage.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Sanusi's theological works are highlighted. How does this shape our understanding of his scholarly priorities?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مؤلفة ومخلف العالمي تنوّعت تأليف الإمام السنوسي في المكتبة الإسلامية ، وهو واحد ممَّن أكثر من التأليف ، ورغم هذا التنوع كانت تآليفه العقدية تاج (۱) انظر ( ثبت أبي جعفر البلوي » ( ص ٤٣٦ ) (۲) انظر » ثبت أبي جعفر البلوي » ( ص ٤٣٧ ) .
His authored works and his [؟] worldly[؟] varied; the writings of Imam al-Sanusi in the Islamic library varied, and he is among those who authored many works, and despite this diversity, his theological writings were the crown (1) See (Thabt Abi Ja‘far al-Balawi, p. 436) (2) See (Thabt Abi Ja‘far al-Balawi, p. 437).
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This short passage is a concluding remark on Imam al-Sanusi's literary output. It states that his writings in the Islamic library are diverse, and he is among those who wrote prolifically. Despite this variety, his theological works (tā’līfuhu al-‘aqdiyya) were the crown—that is, the most distinguished and important. The footnote references likely point to the source of this information, al-Balawi's Thabt. The passage thus emphasizes the centrality of 'aqida in Sanusi's scholarly legacy.

Key Terms
تأليف الإمام السنوسي
تآليفه العقدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage downplays Sanusi's other writings.
Correction: The passage does not downplay his other works; it only highlights the prominence of his theological writings as the crown.

Checkpoint

A) His legal writings
B) His theological writings
C) His Sufi writings
D) His historical writings

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi's theological writings are considered the crown of his diverse literary legacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to evaluate your own learning, what would you say is the 'crown' of your knowledge? Does it align with what is most important in Allah's sight, or with what impresses people?
?

?
The scholars of the past spent their energy on theology even when they were experts in other fields. What is stopping you from giving 'aqeeda the crown in your daily study routine?

Al-Sanusi's Major Creedal Works
List of Sanusi's major creedal works with descriptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major creedal works of Imam al-Sanusi and their significance in Islamic theological literature.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creedal writings are the crown of his scholarly legacy.

Imam Muhammad al-Sanusi is known for his prolific writings, but among them, his works on Islamic creed (aqida) stand out. This passage from his student al-Mallali lists the key texts that established Sanusi's authority in kalam. As you read, notice how each work serves a different purpose, from his first composition at a young age to his comprehensive commentaries. Let's explore these foundational books.

Guided Path

The writings of Imam al-Sanusi diversified in the Islamic library, and he is one of those who authored extensively. Despite this diversity, his creedal works were the crown of his scholarly legacy. His student Sheikh al-Mallali devoted a special chapter to discuss them. You will see before you all the writings he mentioned:

- 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi': it is his first work, and his age when he wrote it was nineteen or eighteen years old, with some uncertainty about that. His story with the gnostic al-Hasan Abarkan and his order to him to hide it until he reaches forty years of age will come later.
- 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-Aqida al-Kubra': it is the first thing he composed in the science of tawhid, and its wording is firm and difficult, as he stated in Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta. (Thus Imam al-Sanusi himself used to name it. As for the addition in its original title (p. 122): 'al-Mukharraja min Zulumat al-Jahl wa Rabaqat al-Taqalid' etc., that is a description of it, not part of its name, as both al-'Ukari and al-Hamidi hold, with al-Mallali confirming this.)
- 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra': this commentary is considered among the most extensive creedal books that the Imam authored.
- 'al-Aqida al-Wusta': it is more concise than al-Kubra and above al-Sughra.
- 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta': it is one of the masterpieces authored in the science of tawhid.

Commentary
This passage lists the major creedal works of Imam al-Sanusi as enumerated by his student al-Mallali. It begins by noting the diversity of Sanusi's writings but emphasizes that his creedal works were the most prominent. Then it lists five works: (1) al-Muqrib, his first composition at a young age, with a story about hiding it until forty. (2) al-Aqida al-Kubra, with a note on its title. (3) The commentary on al-Kubra, which is extensive. (4) al-Aqida al-Wusta, intermediate in length. (5) Its commentary, considered a masterpiece. The footnote about the title of al-Kubra clarifies that the additional descriptive phrase is not part of the official title.

The Medium Creed: al-'Aqida al-Wusta
Sanusi describes the length of al-'Aqida al-Wusta.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-'Aqida al-Wusta is defined by its length relative to Sanusi's other two creeds.

Core Idea
Al-'Aqida al-Wusta is the middle-length creed.

This passage introduces al-'Aqida al-Wusta, one of three creeds by Imam al-Sanusi. It defines its position by comparing its length to the other two, establishing it as the medium creed. The name itself indicates its intermediate status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the comparative terms أخصر (shorter) and فوق (above).
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Analyze the comparisons
Identify what al-Wusta is being compared to: al-Kubra (the major creed) and al-Sughra (the minor creed). Understand that أخصر indicates shorter than al-Kubra, and فوق indicates longer than al-Sughra.
The word فوق literally means 'above' in length, i.e., longer.
3
Reflect on the naming
Consider why this creed is called الوسطى (the middle). How does its length relate to its name?
The name reflects its intermediate position in length between the other two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- العقيدة الوسطى : وهي أخصر من الكبرى» وفوق «الصغرى».
- al-'Aqida al-Wusta: It is shorter than al-Kubra and above al-Sughra.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage briefly describes al-'Aqida al-Wusta, the medium creed, by comparing it to the other two creeds: it is shorter than al-'Aqida al-Kubra and longer (or above) than al-'Aqida al-Sughra. Thus it occupies a middle position in length.

Key Terms
العقيدة الوسطى
الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'al-Wusta' means it is the most balanced or moderate in content.
Correction: The passage only describes its length relative to the other creeds; it does not comment on content or theological balance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā: The Pearl of Sanusi’s Creeds
Introduction to al-Sanusi’s most famous creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity and significance of al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā, the most famous creed of Imam al-Sanusi, and why it is considered unparalleled.

Core Idea
Al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā is the crown jewel of Sanusi’s theological works.

Before diving into the doctrinal content, it is essential to recognize which text holds the highest status among al-Sanusi’s creeds. This lesson introduces the small but mighty al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā, known by several names and celebrated by scholars as the most noble creed ever written. We will explore its titles and the extraordinary reception it received.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the multiple names given to this creed.
2
Note the key names
Identify the four names mentioned for this creed: al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā, Umm al-Barāhīn, Dhāt al-Barāhīn, and al-Sanūsiyya al-Ṣughrā.
‘Umm al-Barāhīn’ means 'Mother of Proofs' – a title indicating its foundational role.
3
Understand its significance
Reflect on the phrase 'the pearl of his creeds' and the quote from al-Malālī declaring it unmatched by any creed before or after.
This establishes the reverence scholars held for this work.
4
Connect to the wider tradition
Consider how this work became central to Islamic education, memorized and commented upon by generations.
The passage highlights its widespread acceptance – a mark of its importance in the Ash‘arī tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-
العقيدة الصغرى » المعروفة بـ «أم البراهين » و « ذات
البراهين » و « السنوسية الصغرى » : وهي درَّةُ «عقائده » ، والمقصودة بقولهم : ( عقيدة السنوسي ) عند الإطلاق ، وكُتب لها من الذيوع ما لم يكتب لغيرها ، وأقبل عليها العلماء شرحاً ، والطلاب حفظاً ودرساً ، قال العلامة الملالي : ( وهي من أجل العقائد ، ولا تعادلها عقيدة من عقائد من تقدم ولا من تأخر ، وقد أشار الشيخ رضي الله عنه إلى ذلك في صدر شرحه لها )(۱)
- 'al-'Aqida al-Sughra' known as 'Umm al-Barahin', 'Dhat al-Barahin', and 'al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra': It is the pearl of his creeds, and the intended meaning when they say 'the creed of al-Sanusi' absolutely. It achieved a widespread popularity not attained by any other. Scholars embraced it for commentary, and students for memorization and study. Al-'Allama al-Malali said: 'It is among the most noble of creeds, and no creed of those who came before or after equals it, and the Shaykh (may Allah be pleased with him) indicated that in the beginning of his commentary on it.'
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage describes al-Sanusi's most famous creed, known by multiple names: al-'Aqida al-Sughra, Umm al-Barahin (Mother of Proofs), Dhat al-Barahin, and al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra. It is called the pearl of his creeds and is the default work referred to when 'Sanusi's creed' is mentioned without qualification. The passage emphasizes its unparalleled popularity among scholars and students, noting that scholars wrote commentaries on it and students memorized and studied it extensively. A quote from al-Malali praises it as the most excellent creed, surpassing all others, and mentions that al-Sanusi himself alluded to this in the introduction to his commentary.

Key Terms

أم البراهين
ذات البراهين
السنوسية الصغرى
العلامة الملالي

Key Takeaway
Al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā is the most famous and revered of al-Sanusi’s creeds, regarded by scholars as the pinnacle of creedal works.

?
Al-Malali said no creed equals this one. If you truly believed that, how would it change the priority you give to memorizing and understanding it? What would you have to rearrange in your life to make that study a reality?
?

Al-Sanusi's Commentary on the Smaller Creed
Introduction to the commentary and its alternative titles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple titles and widespread scholarly reception of al-Sanusi's commentary on al-'Aqida al-Sughra.

Core Idea
A commentary known by many names and studied across the Islamic world.

This passage introduces one of al-Sanusi's most influential works—his commentary on the Smaller Creed. Even before diving into its content, we learn that this commentary achieved remarkable popularity and attracted many scholarly glosses. The alternative titles hint at its comprehensive approach to tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once to get a feel for the language, then read the literal English translation to understand the meaning.
Pay attention to the alternative titles.
2
Identify the three titles
Notice that the commentary is known by three distinct titles. List them and note which one is the primary title.
The titles reveal different emphases of the work.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'Tawhid Ahl al-'Irfan'
Consider what 'Tawhid of the Gnostics' suggests about the commentary's approach and intended audience.
This title links the work to spiritual insight and rational proof.
4
Consider the work's reception
The passage states the commentary achieved widespread popularity and many glosses were written upon it. Think about what this indicates about the work's significance in Islamic scholarship.
Glosses are a sign of intensive study and commentary.

Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage describes the commentary on al-'Aqida al-Sughra, known by several titles: the primary title, 'Sharh al-'Aqida al-Sughra', and alternative titles including 'Tawhid Ahl al-'Irfan wa Ma'rifat Allah wa Rusulihi bi al-Dalil wa al-Burhan' ('The Tawhid of the Gnostics and Knowledge of Allah and His Messengers by Evidence and Proof') and 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin'. The passage notes that this commentary also enjoyed widespread popularity and dissemination, with numerous glosses (hawashi) written upon it, indicating its extensive scholarly reception.

Sanusi's Logic Works and Their Completion Status
Enumeration of Sanusi's logic works with completion notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Sanusi's logical writings and the completion status of each work as recorded by al-Malali.

Core Idea
Sanusi's logic works reveal a scholar's journey from commentary to original composition.

This passage from al-Malali's biographical work lists Sanusi's contributions to logic. It includes commentaries on earlier texts, his own summary, and a self-commentary that became a key resource for later theologians. Notice the pattern of incomplete works and the importance placed on Ibn 'Arafa's difficult style.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of logic works
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the sequence of works: commentary on Ibn al-Yasamin, commentary on al-Khunji's Jumal, commentary on Isaghuji, commentary on Ibn 'Arafa's Mukhtasar, Sanusi's own Mukhtasar, and his commentary on it.
Pay attention to the completion status mentioned for each.
2
Identify completed vs. incomplete works
Notice which works were completed (e.g., commentary on Ibn al-Yasamin was seen in his handwriting, commentary on Isaghuji is attributed to another scholar) and which were incomplete (e.g., commentary on al-Khunji's Jumal, commentary on Ibn 'Arafa's Mukhtasar).
Al-Malali often relies on his own inspection of manuscripts.
3
Reflect on Sanusi's scholarly method
Consider the note about Ibn 'Arafa's difficult style and how Sanusi would seclude himself to solve it. This shows his dedication and the challenges of his work.
The self-commentary on his own Mukhtasar is praised as sufficient for the mutakallim.

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a biographical list of works attributed to Sanusi, as recorded by al-Malali. It begins with a commentary on Ibn al-Yasamin (possibly a mathematical or grammatical work) written in Sanusi's youth, seen in his own handwriting. The first set of works are on logic: a commentary on al-Khunji's 'Jumal' (likely incomplete, with only notebooks seen), a commentary on Isaghuji (attributed to al-Biqa'i, described as large and comprehensive), and a commentary on Ibn 'Arafa's 'Mukhtasar', which was difficult and remained unfinished despite Sanusi's secluded efforts. Then Sanusi's own 'Mukhtasar' in logic, which expanded on al-Khunji's 'Jumal', and his self-commentary on it, which is praised as sufficient for the mutakallim. Footnotes refer to 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' as the source. The next works are on astronomy: 'Umdat dhawī al-albāb wa nuzhat al-ḥussāb', a commentary on his teacher's book on the astrolabe, and a commentary on Ibn Sina's medical poem 'Arjūza', both left incomplete. Then works on Quranic readings: a 'Mukhtasar' in the seven readings (unknown to al-Malali) and a commentary on 'al-Shāṭibiyya al-kubrā' (seen incomplete). Then Maliki fiqh commentaries: on 'al-Mudawwana' (a sufficient portion completed, but completion uncertain) and on 'al-Waghlīsiyya' (only a little done, incomplete). Finally, a poem on inheritance written in youth, opening with rajaz verses on death and inheritance, but whether it was completed is unknown. The passage provides a comprehensive overview of Sanusi's scholarly output across multiple fields, with careful notes on the status of each work.

Key Terms
شرح
مختصر
المنطق
جمل الخونجي
إيساغوجي
المواهب القدسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All listed works are by Sanusi himself.
Correction: The commentary on Isaghuji is explicitly attributed to al-Biqa'i, not Sanusi.

Checkpoint
Which of Sanusi's logic works is described as 'sufficient in this art' and contains most of what a mutakallim needs?
A) Commentary on Ibn 'Arafa's Mukhtasar
B) al-Mukhtasar in logic
C) Commentary on al-Mukhtasar in logic
D) Commentary on Ibn al-Yasamin

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's logic output includes original works and commentaries, with many left unfinished due to difficulty or other reasons, yet his self-commentary became a key text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult subject that makes you want to give up, like Sanusi did with Ibn 'Arafa's work, do you retreat into seclusion and persist, or do you abandon it altogether? What will you commit to today to develop that kind of perseverance?
?
Sanusi left several works incomplete. In your own life, are you more focused on starting many projects or finishing them with depth? Will you identify one unfinished task and resolve to complete it, no matter how hard?
?
The passage shows that even great scholars like Sanusi struggled with understanding. Do you feel ashamed when you don't grasp something immediately? Can you humble yourself and seek help, just as Sanusi sought seclusion and relied on Allah? What step will you take to embrace difficulty as a means of growth?

Sanusi's Works in Astronomy and Quranic Readings
Enumeration of Sanusi's astronomy and Quranic reading works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will identify Sanusi's works in astronomy (astrolabe commentary and commentary on Ibn Sina's poem) and Quranic readings, and know which were completed.

Core Idea
Sanusi's interdisciplinary scholarship spanned astronomy and Quranic sciences, but many projects remained unfinished.

This lesson covers Sanusi's works beyond logic: a commentary on his teacher's book on the astrolabe, a commentary on Ibn Sina's poem, a brief Mukhtasar on the seven Quranic readings, and a commentary on al-Shatibiyya. Again, incompleteness is a recurring theme, as al-Malali notes which works were seen incomplete or unknown.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of works
Read the Arabic and translation to see the astronomy works (astrolabe commentary and commentary on Ibn Sina's Arjuzah) and Quranic readings works (Mukhtasar and commentary on al-Shatibiyya).
Notice that al-Malali is the source for most of these details.
2
Note the completion status
For each work, identify whether it was completed or not. The astrolabe commentary has no explicit status, but the commentary on Ibn Sina's poem was not completed. The Mukhtasar on readings is unknown to al-Malali, and the commentary on al-Shatibiyya was seen incomplete.
The astrolabe work is a commentary on his teacher's book, so it may have been completed.
3
Appreciate Sanusi's breadth
Consider that Sanusi wrote on astronomy (astrolabe) and Quranic readings, showing his range. Even incomplete, these works indicate his scholarly interests.
The astrolabe was an important scientific instrument in Islamic civilization.

Commentary
This passage is a biographical list of works attributed to Sanusi, as recorded by al-Malali. It begins with a commentary on Ibn al-Yasamin (possibly a mathematical or grammatical work) written in Sanusi's youth, seen in his own handwriting. The first set of works are on logic: a commentary on al-Khunji's 'Jumal' (likely incomplete, with only notebooks seen), a commentary on Isaghuji (attributed to al-Biqa'i, described as large and comprehensive), and a commentary on Ibn 'Arafa's 'Mukhtasar', which was difficult and remained unfinished despite Sanusi's secluded efforts. Then Sanusi's own 'Mukhtasar' in logic, which expanded on al-Khunji's 'Jumal', and his self-commentary on it, which is praised as sufficient for the mutakallim. Footnotes refer to 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' as the source. The next works are on astronomy: 'Umdat dhawī al-albāb wa nuzhat al-ḥussāb', a commentary on his teacher's book on the astrolabe, and a commentary on Ibn Sina's medical poem 'Arjūza', both left incomplete. Then works on Quranic readings: a 'Mukhtasar' in the seven readings (unknown to al-Malali) and a commentary on 'al-Shāṭibiyya al-kubrā' (seen incomplete). Then Maliki fiqh commentaries: on 'al-Mudawwana' (a sufficient portion completed, but completion uncertain) and on 'al-Waghlīsiyya' (only a little done, incomplete). Finally, a poem on inheritance written in youth, opening with rajaz verses on death and inheritance, but whether it was completed is unknown. The passage provides a comprehensive overview of Sanusi's scholarly output across multiple fields, with careful notes on the status of each work.

Sanusi's Fiqh Commentaries and Inheritance Poem
Enumeration of Sanusi's fiqh works and a poem on inheritance.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Sanusi's fiqh commentaries were extensive but incomplete, and he also composed a poem on inheritance in his youth.

The final part of the biographical list covers Sanusi's Maliki fiqh works: a commentary on al-Mudawwana (partially completed) and a commentary on al-Waghlisiyya (only a little done). Then a poem on inheritance written in his youth, but it is unclear if it was finished. This lesson shows Sanusi's dedication to fiqh even as many works remained incomplete.

Sanusi's Major Works and the True Authorship of Haqa'iq al-Sanusi
Lists Sanusi's works and clarifies Haqa'iq authorship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list Imam Sanusi's major works and understand the correct attribution of the treatise Haqa'iq al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
Sanusi's writings span multiple disciplines, and one work is often misattributed.

This lesson presents a biographical note on Imam Sanusi's literary output. It first lists his known compositions, including commentaries, tafsir, and other writings. It then addresses a common confusion: the authorship of a well-known treatise on definitions, Haqa'iq al-Sanusi, clarifying that it was compiled by his teacher using Sanusi's material.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words introduce the works.
Notice the shift from listing works to discussing Haqa'iq.
2
Identify the two sections
Notice where the enumeration of works ends and the discussion of Haqa'iq al-Sanusi begins. The transition is marked by 'ثمَّ كتب فيها تأمل عند نسبتها إليه'.
3
Understand the attribution of Haqa'iq
Focus on why the work is often misattributed to Sanusi: the content is his, but the compiler is his teacher al-Tha‘ālibī.
This addresses a common misconception.

شرح جواهر العلوم ( في علم الكلام : والأصل للعلامة العضد الإيجي ، وهو على منهج الإمام البيضاوي في « طوالع الأنوار » ، بل بلغت صعوبته حتى قال الشيخ الإمام السنوسي : ( البيضاوي نقطة هذا الكتاب ((٢)

وثَمَّ كتب فيها تأمل عند نسبتها إليه ، فليقع الكلام عليها : حقائق السنوسي ، ويعرف أيضاً بـ « الحقائق في التعريفات (۲) : ما تراه من الحدود والتعريفات في هذه الرسالة جلُّهُ للإمام السنوسي ، ولكنه ليس هو المؤلف لهذه الرسالة ، وإنما هي لشيخه الإمام الحافظ عبد الرحمن الثعالبي الهواري ، وقد رسالته هذه من شرح العقيدة الصغرى ( لتلميذه الإمام السنوسي هذا الشرح الذي اعتنى فيه الإمام ببيان كل مصطلح دائر في علم الكلام ، ويظهر أنَّ جمعها قد راق لشيخه الإمام الثعالبي ، ولكن لما كانت هذه المادة للإمام السنوسي وهَم بعضهم فنسبها إليه ، هذا ما يظهر والعلم عند الله تعالى
والناظر في النسخ المنتشرة لهذه ( الحقائق » سيرى اختلافاً كثيراً من حيث الزيادة والنقصان ، ولعله قد وقعت محاولات مقارنة مع كتب الإمام السنوسي ألجأت إلى زيادة بعض التعريفات ، وأيّاً ما كان الأمر فالكتاب نافع ومفيد
توحيد أهل العرفان ، ومعرفة الله ورسله بالدليل والبرهان
Al-Durr al-Manẓūm fī Sharḥ Qawā‘id Ibn Ājurrūm: it is a commentary on al-Ājurrūmiyya in the science of grammar. Al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: 'I saw it in his handwriting, complete.' (1)
Sharḥ Jawāhir al-‘Ulūm (in the science of kalām): the original is by al-‘Allāma al-‘Aḍud al-Ījī, and it follows the method of Imām al-Bayḍāwī in Ṭawāli‘ al-Anwār; indeed its difficulty reached such a level that al-Shaykh al-Imām al-Sanūsī said: 'al-Bayḍāwī is a dot in this book.' (2)
Tafsīr al-Qur’ān al-‘Azīz: he reached in it the saying of the Exalted from Sūrat al-Baqara: 'Those are upon guidance from their Lord, and those are the successful' [al-Baqara: 5].
Tafsīr Sūrat Ṣād and what follows it to the end of the Noble Qur’ān: al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: 'And I do not know to which sūrahs he reached.' (3)
Al-Shaykh al-‘Allāma al-Mallālī, the historian of the biography of Imām al-Sanūsī, said after these writings: 'This is what we know that God has brought forth all of his compositions, may God be pleased with him; and add to that: what he wrote of answers to the questions that came to him most of the time, and some answers it is good to count them among his compositions, may God be pleased with him, due to their size and independence, and what he wrote of exhortations, bequests, letters, and talismans that were sought from him, and what he copied by hand of the writings of scholars and the collections of the early ones.' (1)
Then there are books in which there is reflection when attributing them to him, so let discussion fall on them: Ḥaqā’iq al-Sanūsī, also known as 'al-Ḥaqā’iq fī al-Ta‘rīfāt' (2): what you see of definitions in this treatise, most of it is by Imām al-Sanūsī, but he is not the author of this treatise; rather it is by his shaykh, Imām al-Ḥāfiẓ ‘Abd al-Raḥmān al-Tha‘ālibī al-Hawārī, and his treatise is part of his commentary on al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā (for his student Imām al-Sanūsī, this commentary in which the Imām took care to explain every term current in the science of kalām). And it appears that its collection pleased his shaykh Imām al-Tha‘ālibī, but since this material belonged to Imām al-Sanūsī, some people mistakenly attributed it to him. This is what appears, and knowledge is with God Almighty.
And the one who examines the widespread copies of this 'al-Ḥaqā’iq' will see much variation in terms of addition and omission, and perhaps comparative attempts with the books of Imām al-Sanūsī occurred that forced the addition of some definitions. Whatever the case, the book is beneficial and useful.
Tawḥīd Ahl al-‘Irfān, wa-Ma‘rifat Allāh wa-Rusulihi bi-al-Dalīl wa-al-Burhān.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographical note on Imam al-Sanusi's written works. It begins by listing his known compositions:
1. Al-Durr al-Manẓūm fī Sharḥ Qawā‘id Ibn Ājurrūm – a commentary on the famous grammar work al-Ājurrūmiyya. Al-Shaykh al-Mallālī confirms seeing a complete manuscript in Sanusi's handwriting.
2. Sharḥ Jawāhir al-‘Ulūm – a commentary in kalām based on al-Ījī's original, following al-Bayḍāwī's method in Ṭawāli‘ al-Anwār. Sanusi remarked that al-Bayḍāwī's work is like a dot compared to this commentary, indicating its superiority or comprehensiveness.
3. Tafsīr al-Qur’ān al-‘Azīz – an incomplete tafsir reaching only to verse 5 of Sūrat al-Baqara.
4. Tafsīr Sūrat Ṣād to the end of the Qur’ān – but its completion is unknown.
Then al-Mallālī adds other types of writings: answers to questions, exhortations, bequests, letters, talismans, and manuscripts copied by Sanusi.
The passage then addresses a work whose attribution is uncertain: Ḥaqā’iq al-Sanūsī (or al-Ḥaqā’iq fī al-Ta‘rīfāt). It clarifies that most of the definitions in this treatise are by Sanusi, but the compiler is actually his teacher ‘Abd al-Raḥmān al-Tha‘ālibī, who collected them from Sanusi's commentary on al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā. Because the content is Sanusi's, some have mistakenly attributed the whole book to him. The text notes that copies vary significantly, possibly due to later additions from Sanusi's other works. Despite this, the book is deemed beneficial.
Finally, it mentions another work: Tawḥīd Ahl al-‘Irfān, wa-Ma‘rifat Allāh wa-Rusulihi bi-al-Dalīl wa-al-Burhān, likely a treatise on tawḥīd.

طوالع الأنوار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ḥaqā’iq al-Sanūsī is entirely authored by Imam Sanusi.
Correction: The work is a compilation by his teacher al-Tha‘ālibī, though it heavily uses Sanusi's definitions.

Checkpoint
Why is the work 'Ḥaqā’iq al-Sanūsī' often attributed to Imam Sanusi, according to this passage?
A) Because Sanusi himself wrote it in his own handwriting.
B) Because the definitions in it are mostly by Sanusi, so some mistakenly think he authored the whole compilation.
C) Because it is a commentary on Sanusi's own 'al-‘Aqīda al-Ṣughrā'.
D) Because al-Tha‘ālibī was Sanusi's student and copied his work.

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi authored works in grammar, kalām, and tafsir, but the treatise Haqa'iq al-Sanusi is actually a compilation by his teacher al-Tha‘ālibī, though it draws heavily from Sanusi's own definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a book on Islamic knowledge, do you ever stop to verify who truly authored it, or do you accept attributions blindly? This lesson reveals a famous work is not by the scholar it is named after. How will you change your approach to knowing the sources of what you study? Commit to checking one book's authorship this week.
?

?
